So to cover those points, we need a horizontal line, which is not sunny. So we cannot cover any row (with multiple points) with a sunny line. Similarly, we cannot cover any column with a sunny line. However we could cover some points on a row with a sunny line, but if the row has many points, it's unlikely that a sunny line passes through all of them, but it could pass through some of them. For example, line y = 2x passes through (1,2) and (2,4) etc. So it passes through (1,2) which is on row y=2, and (2,4) not in S. So it's okay. But we need to cover all points. So we need to design n lines (some sunny, some not) covering all points. The question is to determine all k (sunny count) such that such a covering exists. So we need to find the set of achievable k.

Observation: The points (a,b) with a,b positive, a+b в‰¤ n+1. Consider the lines:

- For each b from 1 to n, the horizontal line y = b covers points (a,b) with a в‰¤ n+1-b.
- For each a from 1 to n, the vertical line x = a covers points (a,b) with b в‰¤ n+1-a.
- For each d from 2 to n+1, the diagonal line x+y = d covers points (a,b) with a+b = d, a,b в‰Ґ 1.

Now, we have n lines total. Perhaps we can cover all points using n lines that are all non-sunny (like all horizontal, or all vertical, or all diagonal). That's k=0.

Can we cover all points using n lines with exactly 1 sunny line? That would be n-1 non-sunny lines. Is that possible? We could use, say, n-1 horizontal lines to cover most rows, and then one sunny line to cover the remaining points that are not covered by those horizontal lines. But we have n lines total. If we take n-1 horizontal lines, which rows? The points in S have y from 1 to n. So there are n rows. Taking n-1 horizontal lines covers all but one row. The uncovered row points (a,b) with that y) would need to be covered by the sunny line. But can a single sunny line cover an entire row (multiple points) with that y? As argued, to cover multiple points with same y, the line must be horizontal. A sunny line cannot be horizontal. So it cannot cover all points of that row. However it could cover some points, but we need to cover all points in that row. Since we have only n lines total, and we have n-1 horizontal lines covering all but one row, we still need to cover the remaining points (the entire row). The sunny line cannot cover all points of that row because it would need to be horizontal. But perhaps we could use a combination of non-sunny lines that are not all horizontal, like some vertical and some diagonals, to cover points efficiently, and then use some sunny lines to cover the rest. We need to find a configuration with exactly k sunny lines. So we need to consider whether there is a lower bound on the number of non-sunny lines required to cover S, perhaps because certain points cannot be covered by sunny lines unless we have enough non-sunny lines. Let's explore.

Let L be a sunny line. Since it is not parallel to x-axis, y-axis, or x+y=0, its slope is not 0, в€ћ, -1. So it can intersect each of those families in some way. Could a sunny line cover multiple points of S? Yes. For example, a line with slope 2 can pass through (1,2) and (2,4) but (2,4) not in S for n=3. So for larger n, there might be sunny lines that pass through many points. Let's examine the structure of S. Points (a,b) with a+b в‰¤ n+1. Consider a line with equation y = mx + c. For it to contain a point (a,b) with a,b positive integers, we need b = ma + c. If m is rational, we can have many integer solutions. But we are not limited to integer slopes; any real slope is allowed. However to cover many integer points, the line must pass through them. So if we want a sunny line to cover many points, we could choose a line that passes through many lattice points. The set of lattice points that lie on a line with rational slope (or irrational) is limited. For a line to contain many lattice points, it must be of the form y = (p/q)x + b where b is rational such that all points are integer. Actually the line passing through two integer points has rational slope (or infinite). So if we want a line to contain many integer points (like a grid), it must be that all those points are collinear with rational slope (or vertical). So sunny lines with rational slope (not 0, в€ћ, -1) can contain many integer points, but they will be lines with slope like 1/2, 2, 1/3, etc. For example, line y = (1/2)x + 1/2 passes through (1,1) and (3,2) etc. So possible. So we can try to cover S with sunny lines that are not axis-aligned. However we need to cover all points. Since there are many points, we may need many lines. The total number of lines is n. So perhaps the only way to cover all points with n lines is to use lines that are all non-sunny (i.e., from those three families). But we could also mix, but maybe some sunny lines are needed to cover points that are not covered by non-sunny lines if we use fewer than n non-sunny lines. But we can always use n non-sunny lines (all horizontal) and have k=0. So k=0 is achievable. For k=n, we need all sunny lines. Is it possible? Let's test small n with n=3. We need 3 sunny lines covering all 6 points. Let's try to construct manually. We have points: (1,1),(1,2),(1,3),(2,1),(2,2),(3,1). We need 3 lines, all sunny. Let's consider using lines that are not horizontal, vertical, or slope -1. Could we use lines like:

L1: passes through (1,1),(2,2) (slope 1) -> sunny. L2: passes through (1,2),(2,1) (slope -1) -> not sunny. L3: passes through (1,3),(3,1) (slope -1) -> not sunny. So that's not good.

Maybe use lines with slope 2: L1: y = 2x - 1? That passes through (1,1) and (2,3) not in S. L2: y = 2x? passes through (1,2). L3: y = 2x - 2? passes through (1,0) no. Not covering all.

Maybe use lines with slope 1/2: L1: y = (1/2)x + 1/2 passes through (1,1),(3,2) but (3,2) sum=5>4 not in S. So covers (1,1). L2: y = (1/2)x + 1 passes through (1,1.5) no integer. Not many.

Maybe use lines with slope -2: L1: y = -2x + 4 passes through (1,2),(2,0) not in S, so covers (1,2). L2: y = -2x + 5 passes through (1,3),(2,1) but (2,1) sum=3 в‰¤4, so covers (1,3) and (2,1). That's two points. L3: maybe y = -2x + 3 passes through (1,1),(2,-1) no. So covers (1,1). But we need to cover (2,2) as well. (2,2) sum=4, difference 0. Does any of these lines cover (2,2)? L1 y=-2x+4 -> at x=2, y=0 not. L2 y=-2x+5 -> at x=2, y=1 not. L3 not. So not covered.

Maybe we can use lines that are not through many points but we can cover all with 3 lines by taking lines that are not parallel to each other, perhaps like:

L1: y = 2x - 1 (covers (1,1))
L2: y = 2x (covers (1,2))
L3: y = 2x + 1 (covers (1,3))? At x=1, y=3 -> (1,3). That's three lines, but they cover only points with x=1, and (2,2) missing. We could also cover (2,2) with a line like y = (1/2)x + 1.5? Not integer.

But we can also use lines that are not necessarily through integer points, but they can cover points that are not collinear with integer slopes. For example, a line passing through (1,1) and (2,2) is slope 1, sunny. That's good. But we need to cover all points. Could we cover (2,1) with a line that is not horizontal, not vertical, not slope -1, and also covers other points? For (2,1) and (3,1) are collinear horizontally, but that line is not sunny. So we cannot cover both (2,1) and (3,1) with a sunny line. But we could cover (2,1) with a sunny line that also covers (1,2) perhaps, if they are collinear. (1,2) to (2,1) is slope -1, not sunny. So that's not allowed. (2,1) and (1,3) slope? (1,3) to (2,1) slope -2, not -1, so that's allowed. (2,1) and (3,1) are horizontal. So maybe we can design a sunny line that passes through (2,1) and (1,3). That line has slope (1-3)/(2-1) = -2, which is allowed. So such a line covers (2,1) and (1,3). That's two points. Similarly, a line through (2,2) and (1,1) is slope 1, covers (1,1),(2,2). That's two points. Then we need to cover (1,2) and (3,1) and maybe (2,1) and (2,2) etc. Let's attempt to cover all 6 points with 3 sunny lines:

Possible lines:

L1: passes through (1,1) and (2,2) (slope 1) -> covers (1,1),(2,2).
L2: passes through (1,2) and (2,1) (slope -1) -> not sunny. So cannot use that.
L3: passes through (1,3) and (3,1) (slope -1) -> not sunny.
L4: passes through (2,1) and (1,3) (slope -2) -> covers (2,1),(1,3).
L5: passes through (2,1) and (3,1) (horizontal) -> not sunny.
L6: passes through (1,2) and (3,1) slope (1-2)/(3-1) = -1/2 -> covers (1,2),(3,1) maybe also (2,?) not. So that's allowed.

So we have candidate sunny lines: slope 1 line L1 (covers (1,1),(2,2)); slope -2 line L4 (covers (2,1),(1,3)); slope -1/2 line L6 (covers (1,2),(3,1)). That's three lines. Check coverage:

- (1,1): L1 covers.
- (1,2): L6 covers.
- (1,3): L4 covers.
- (2,1): L4 covers.
- (2,2): L1 covers.
- (3,1): L6 covers.

All covered! So indeed we have a covering of S with 3 sunny lines (all sunny). So k = n = 3 is achievable. Good.

Thus for n=3, both k=0 and k=3 are possible. What about k=1 or k=2? Possibly also possible. Let's test n=3 with k=1. That would be 2 non-sunny lines and 1 sunny line. Can we cover S with, say, 2 non-sunny lines (maybe horizontal and vertical) and 1 sunny line? We need to cover all 6 points. Let's try to design.

We have 3 lines total. We could use 2 horizontal lines (covering rows y=1 and y=2) and 1 sunny line to cover the remaining points (row y=3 and points (2,1),(3,1) which are in rows y=1,2? Actually (2,1) is in row y=1; (3,1) is in row y=1; (1,3) is row y=3. So if we take horizontal lines y=1 and y=2, they cover points:

y=1: (1,1),(2,1),(3,1)
y=2: (1,2),(2,2)

That's 5 points. The missing point is (1,3). Then the sunny line must cover (1,3). But can a sunny line cover just that point and possibly others? It's okay if it covers only that point, but we need to cover all points, so we need to cover (1,3). A single sunny line could pass through (1,3) and maybe also cover other points, but we only have one sunny line; we can choose any line. So we can take a sunny line passing through (1,3) and also through, say, (2,2) (but (2,2) is already covered, that's fine). However we must ensure that the sunny line is not horizontal, vertical, or slope -1. So we can choose line through (1,3) and (2,2). That's slope (2-3)/(2-1) = -1, which is not sunny. So not allowed. Through (1,3) and (3,1) slope -1, not sunny. Through (1,3) and (2,1) slope (1-3)/(2-1) = -2, which is allowed. That line covers (1,3) and (2,1). But (2,1) is already covered by horizontal y=1, but that's okay. So we can have sunny line L: y = -2x + 5? Actually passes through (1,3) and (2,1). That's slope -2, allowed. So we can take:

- L1 (non-sunny horizontal y=1) covers (1,1),(2,1),(3,1)
- L2 (non-sunny horizontal y=2) covers (1,2),(2,2)
- L3 (sunny) covers (1,3),(2,1) (but (2,1) already covered). So all points covered. However note: L3 must be distinct from L1 and L2. L3 is not horizontal, so distinct. Also it is sunny (slope -2). So k=1 is possible.

But is there any issue with "exactly k of the n lines are sunny"? That means we need exactly 1 sunny line, the other 2 are non-sunny. So this configuration works. So k=1 is possible for n=3.

What about k=2? That would be 1 non-sunny line and 2 sunny lines. Let's try to design. We need to cover all 6 points with 3 lines, where exactly 2 are sunny. Could we take, say, 1 horizontal line y=1 (non-sunny) covering (1,1),(2,1),(3,1). Then we need 2 sunny lines to cover the remaining points: (1,2),(1,3),(2,2). Can we cover those with 2 sunny lines? For example, line through (1,2) and (2,2) is horizontal (not sunny). So not allowed. Through (1,2) and (1,3) is vertical (not sunny). Through (1,2) and (2,2) is horizontal; through (1,2) and (3,1) slope (1-2)/(3-1) = -1/2, allowed; through (1,3) and (2,2) slope -1, not allowed; through (1,3) and (3,1) slope -1, not allowed; through (2,2) and (3,1) slope (1-2)/(3-2) = -1, not allowed. So we need two sunny lines covering those three points. Could we cover all three points with one sunny line? No, because they are not collinear in a sunny way. So we need two sunny lines. Possibly one sunny line covering (1,2) and (2,2) is not allowed, but we could cover (1,2) with a sunny line that also covers (1,3) (vertical) not allowed; or cover (1,2) with a sunny line that covers (2,2) but that's horizontal. So maybe we can cover (1,2) and (1,3) with a sunny line? That would be vertical, not allowed. So to cover (1,2) and (1,3), we would need a vertical line, which is not sunny. So they cannot be covered by a sunny line. However we could cover (1,2) and (1,3) by two different sunny lines, but they would be lines that are not vertical; but any line passing through (1,2) and (1,3) is vertical, so there is no other line containing both because they have the same x-coordinate. So to cover both (1,2) and (1,3), we must use a vertical line. So that would be non-sunny. Thus if we have only 1 non-sunny line, we cannot cover both (1,2) and (1,3) because we would need a vertical line (non-sunny) to cover them, and we already used 1 non-sunny line. But maybe we can cover (1,2) and (1,3) using the same sunny line? As argued, no. So they must be covered by non-sunny lines. Similarly, (2,2) and (3,1) etc. Let's analyze systematically.

We need to see whether k=2 is possible for n=3. Let's try to construct with 2 sunny lines and 1 non-sunny line. The non-sunny line could be horizontal or vertical or slope -1. We need to cover all points. The points (1,2) and (1,3) are on a vertical line x=1. That line is not sunny. So if we include that vertical line as non-sunny, then we cover both points. That's one non-sunny line. Then we need to cover the remaining points (1,1),(2,1),(2,2),(3,1). Can we cover those with 2 sunny lines? Let's see.

Points: (1,1),(2,1),(2,2),(3,1). That's 4 points. We have 2 sunny lines. We need to cover all. Perhaps we can use a sunny line that covers (1,1),(2,2) (slope 1). That's one sunny line. Then the remaining points (2,1),(3,1) are on a horizontal line y=1, which is not sunny. But we could cover (2,1) and (3,1) with a sunny line? That would require a line that is not horizontal, but (2,1) and (3,1) are collinear horizontally, any line passing through them is horizontal. So cannot be sunny. So we need to cover (2,1) and (3,1) with the second sunny line. But as argued, any line containing both (2,1) and (3,1) is horizontal (since they have same y), so not sunny. So we cannot cover both with a single sunny line. Could we cover (2,1) with one sunny line and (3,1) with another sunny line? But we only have two sunny lines total. We already used one sunny line covering (1,1),(2,2). Then we have one sunny line left. That sunny line could cover, say, (2,1) and also maybe (3,1) if they are collinear with a non-horizontal line? But they are not collinear with any non-horizontal line because any line passing through (2,1) and (3,1) is horizontal. So a sunny line cannot contain both. However a sunny line could contain (2,1) and not (3,1), and then we need to cover (3,1) with something else, but we have only two sunny lines, and we already used one, leaving one sunny line. That one sunny line could cover (3,1) but then (2,1) would be uncovered. So not possible. Could we use a different set of sunny lines? For example, cover (2,1) and (3,1) with a single sunny line? Not allowed. So maybe we can cover all points with 2 sunny lines and 1 non-sunny line by choosing a different non-sunny line, e.g., a diagonal (slope -1) that covers some points, and then two sunny lines cover the rest. Let's try.

We have 6 points. Suppose we take non-sunny line: x+y=4 (slope -1) covering (1,3),(2,2),(3,1). That's 3 points. Then we need to cover (1,1),(1,2),(2,1) with 2 sunny lines. (1,1) and (2,1) are on y=1, horizontal. So cannot be covered by a sunny line together. (1,2) is vertical with (1,1). So we could cover (1,1) and (1,2) with a vertical line (non-sunny). But we already used one non-sunny line. So not allowed. Could we cover (1,1) and (2,1) with a sunny line? That's horizontal, not allowed. So need to design.

Perhaps we can use a non-sunny line that is horizontal covering some points, and then two sunny lines covering the rest. For n=3, we already saw that with 2 horizontal lines (y=1,y=2) we cover 5 points, missing (1,3). Then one sunny line covers (1,3). That's k=1. To get k=2, we need two sunny lines and one non-sunny. So we could use a non-sunny line that is not horizontal, maybe a vertical line covering (1,1),(1,2),(1,3) (x=1). That's one non-sunny line covering 3 points. Then we have two sunny lines to cover (2,1),(2,2),(3,1). That's 3 points. Can we cover those with two sunny lines? Possibly we can use a sunny line covering (2,1) and (3,1) but that's horizontal, not allowed. Or cover (2,1) and (2,2) (horizontal) not allowed. Or cover (2,2) and (3,1) slope -1 (not allowed). So we need to see if two sunny lines can cover those three points. Let's list the points: (2,1), (2,2), (3,1). Consider sunny line L1: passes through (2,2) and (3,1) slope -1 (not allowed). So not. L1: passes through (2,1) and (3,1) horizontal. So not. L1: passes through (2,2) and (2,1) horizontal. So not. L1: passes through (2,2) and (2,1) horizontal. So all lines connecting pairs are either horizontal or slope -1. So none are sunny. Therefore any line that contains at least two of those points is not sunny. So we need to cover them with sunny lines. Since we have two sunny lines, we could have each sunny line contain one of the points, but we need to cover all three points. However a line can contain at most one of these points if it's sunny, because any line containing two of them is not sunny. So to cover three points, we would need at least three lines (if each line covers at most one point). But we only have two sunny lines. So it's impossible. Therefore k=2 is not possible for n=3. So for n=3, achievable k are 0,1,3. (We haven't checked k=2? Actually we concluded impossible. Let's double-check: Could we cover (2,1),(2,2),(3,1) with two sunny lines such that one line covers, say, (2,1) and (2,2) (horizontal) not sunny. So can't. Could we cover (2,1) and (3,1) with a sunny line? No, horizontal. Could we cover (2,2) and (3,1) with a sunny line? That's slope -1, not sunny. So any line containing any two of those points is not sunny. Therefore each sunny line can contain at most one of those points. So with two sunny lines, at most two points can be covered. But there are three points, so impossible. So k=2 impossible.

Thus for n=3, k can be 0,1,3.

Now we need to generalize for n>=3. The problem says "Determine all nonnegative integers k such that there exist n distinct lines in the plane satisfying both of the following: ..." So we need to find the set of k (depending on n) for which such a configuration exists. It might be something like: all k except those between 1 and n-1? Or maybe k can be any integer from 0 to n, but with some restrictions depending on parity or something. Let's explore.

We have S of size N = n(n+1)/2. We have n lines. The lines are distinct. The condition about sunny lines: a line is sunny if it is not parallel to x-axis, y-axis, or line x+y=0. So sunny lines are those with slopes not equal to 0, в€ћ, -1. So they can have any other slope. They can be any lines except those three directions.

Now, we need to determine possible numbers of sunny lines among the n lines. We can try to construct examples with various k. The constructions we gave for n=3: k=0 (all horizontal), k=1 (two horizontal, one sunny), k=3 (three sunny). We need to see if for general n, k=0 is always possible (take all n horizontal lines). Similarly, k=n is possible? For n=3 we found a sunny covering with 3 lines. For larger n, can we cover S with n sunny lines? We need to design n distinct sunny lines covering all points in S. That seems plausible by taking lines of slope 1 (sunny) and then some other sunny lines to cover the remaining points, similar to the n=3 construction but scaled. The n=3 construction used three lines with slopes 1, -2, -1/2. That's a specific set. For n=4, we could perhaps find a set of 4 sunny lines covering all points. Let's test. S for n=4: points (a,b) with a,b в‰Ґ 1, a+b в‰¤ 5. Points: (1,1),(1,2),(1,3),(1,4),(2,1),(2,2),(2,3),(3,1),(3,2),(4,1). That's 10 points. We need 4 lines, all sunny. Can we cover with 4 sunny lines? Possibly yes, by taking lines that are not parallel to those three. We could try to replicate the pattern: use a line of slope 1 (covers points where a=b), a line of slope -2 (covers some), and a line of slope -1/2, but we need 4 lines. The triangle shape is larger. Maybe we can cover all points with n sunny lines by using lines of slopes 1, -2, -1/2, and then some other lines. But we need to find a systematic construction.

Alternatively, we could cover S with n sunny lines by taking lines that are all of the form y = mx + c where m is a rational number with denominator 1? Actually slopes like 2, 3, etc. But then they might pass through many points. Let's think about covering the triangular grid with lines of slope 1. That's like covering the "main diagonal" points. To cover all points, we need lines that cover rows, columns, diagonals. Since we have n lines, and there are n rows, n columns, n diagonals (x+y constant). But only sunny lines are allowed, so we cannot use horizontal, vertical, or slope -1 lines. So we need to find a covering with n lines that are not those. The examples we have for n=3 used lines of slope 1, -2, -1/2. Those lines are not axis-aligned. Could we generalize to any n? Maybe we can use lines with slopes that are distinct and not forbidden, such that each line covers a distinct set of points and collectively cover S. Since there are n lines, we can try to cover the n points on the "boundary" in some pattern.

Let's analyze the covering constraints more deeply. The points are lattice points in a triangle. We want to cover them with n lines. The lines are distinct. The condition "exactly k of the n lines are sunny" means we can choose any set of lines with that property.

We need to find all k for which there exists a set of n distinct lines covering S, and exactly k are sunny.

Observations:

- If we use a non-sunny line (horizontal, vertical, or slope -1), it is very effective at covering many points (like a row, column, or diagonal). So to minimize the number of sunny lines, we can use many non-sunny lines. But we want to maximize sunny lines (k). The maximum possible sunny lines is n (all sunny). So we need to determine whether we can cover S with n sunny lines. If yes, then k=n is achievable. If not, then the maximum achievable k is something less. We also need to see if intermediate k are achievable.

Let's test n=4 with an attempt to find 4 sunny lines covering all points. Maybe we can extend the n=3 construction. In n=3, we had points (1,1),(1,2),(1,3),(2,1),(2,2),(3,1). We used lines:

- L1: through (1,1) and (2,2) (slope 1)
- L2: through (2,1) and (1,3) (slope -2)
- L3: through (1,2) and (3,1) (slope -1/2)

Let's list the points covered by each:

L1 covers (1,1),(2,2). L2 covers (2,1),(1,3). L3 covers (1,2),(3,1). So each line covers two points, and the three lines cover all six points.

For n=4, S has points: (1,1),(1,2),(1,3),(1,4),(2,1),(2,2),(2,3),(3,1),(3,2),(4,1). That's 10 points. We need 4 sunny lines. Can we design a covering where each sunny line covers some points, perhaps some lines cover more than 2 points. For instance, line of slope 1 (y=x) covers points with a=b: (1,1),(2,2),(3,3) not in S (3,3 sum 6 >5), so only (1,1),(2,2). That's 2 points. Line of slope -2 (y=-2x+ c) could cover some points. For n=4, we could try lines:

- L1 (slope 1): covers (1,1),(2,2). 
- L2 (slope -2): choose line passing through (1,4) and (2,2) perhaps? (1,4) to (2,2) slope -2. That line also passes through (3,0) not in S. So covers (1,4) and (2,2). But (2,2) already covered by L1.
- L3 (slope -1/2): choose line passing through (1,3) and (3,1) maybe? (1,3) to (3,1) slope (1-3)/(3-1) = -2/2 = -1, not -1/2. Actually slope -1. So not allowed. Through (1,2) and (3,2) horizontal. Not allowed. Through (1,3) and (3,2) slope (2-3)/(3-1) = -1/2, that's allowed. That line passes through (1,3) and (3,2). Also (2,? ) at x=2, y = (?) Solve: line with slope -1/2, passes through (1,3). Equation: y = -1/2 (x-1) + 3 = -1/2 x + 1/2 + 3 = -1/2 x + 3.5. Then at x=2, y = -1 + 3.5 = 2.5 (not integer). So covers (1,3) and (3,2). (3,2) is in S. That's two points. We also have points (2,1),(2,3),(3,1),(4,1). Not covered yet. Could we cover with another sunny line? We have 4 lines total. So far we used 3 lines (L1,L2,L3) covering some points. We need to cover the rest with a 4th sunny line. But we need to check coverage of all points. Let's list all points in S and see if they are covered by L1,L2,L3 as defined.

Define:

L1: y = x (slope 1). Covers (1,1),(2,2).

L2: line through (1,4) and (2,2) with slope -2. Equation: y = -2x + 6? Since passes (1,4) gives 4 = -2*1 + c => c=6. So y = -2x + 6. At x=2, y=2. At x=3, y=0. At x=4, y=-2. So covers (1,4),(2,2). Also (2,2) already covered.

L3: line through (1,3) and (3,2) with slope -1/2. Equation: slope -1/2, passes (1,3). y = -1/2 (x-1) + 3 = -1/2 x + 1/2 + 3 = -1/2 x + 3.5. At x=2, y = -1 + 3.5 = 2.5 not integer. So covers (1,3) and (3,2). (3,2) is in S.

Now uncovered points in S: (1,2),(2,1),(2,3),(3,1),(4,1). That's 5 points. Could we cover these with a single sunny line? That would be 4th line. But a line can cover many points. Can we find a sunny line passing through, say, (2,1) and (4,1) horizontal, not sunny. Through (2,1) and (3,2) slope (2-1)/(3-2)=1, which is sunny, but that line also passes through (1,0) not in S. It covers (2,1) and (3,2) and (1,0). So would cover (2,1) and (3,2) but (3,2) already covered. That's okay. But still (1,2),(2,3),(4,1) remain. Could we cover (2,3) and (4,1) slope (1-3)/(4-2) = -2/2 = -1, not allowed. Through (2,3) and (1,2) slope (2-3)/(1-2) = -1/-1 = 1, allowed. That line covers (2,3),(1,2) and also (3,4) not in S. That's good. But we need to cover (4,1) as well. So maybe we can choose a line that passes through (4,1) and (2,3) slope -1 (not allowed). So cannot cover both. Could we cover (1,2),(2,3),(4,1) with a single sunny line? They are not collinear. So need multiple lines.

Thus with 4 lines, we cannot cover all points if we restrict to sunny lines. But perhaps we can design a different set of 4 sunny lines covering all 10 points. Let's attempt a systematic approach.

We need to cover S, which is a set of integer points in a triangle. The lines are not required to be distinct in terms of slope or type, but they must be distinct lines. We can choose any lines. For covering with all sunny lines, we can think about using lines that are not parallel to the three forbidden directions. The set of points S has some structure. Perhaps we can cover S with n lines that are all of the form y = mx + b with m being a rational number where denominator is something, and intercepts chosen so that each line covers a "row" or "column" of points but not exactly; we can use lines with slope 1 that cover the diagonal points, and then use lines with slope 2 that cover other points, etc. But we need to check feasibility.

Alternate viewpoint: The condition that a line is sunny is that its slope is not 0, в€ћ, or -1. So we can have lines with slope 1, 2, -2, 1/2, etc. So maybe we can cover S with n sunny lines by taking, for each b from 1 to n, a line that passes through all points with y-coordinate b (i.e., horizontal) is not sunny. So we cannot use that. But we could use lines that are nearly horizontal but with a small slope, but they won't cover many points because the points are on integer lattice, and a line with slope not 0 will intersect each horizontal line at most once. So to cover many points, it's more efficient to have lines that are axis-aligned or with slope -1. So perhaps for n>3, it's impossible to cover S with n sunny lines. Let's test n=4 with a computer-like mental search. But we can reason combinatorially.

We have n lines. The total number of points is N = n(n+1)/2. If each line covers at most some number of points, then with n lines we need to cover N points. But we can have overlap. However, if we want to cover all points with sunny lines, each line can contain at most some number of points from S. Let's find an upper bound on the number of points from S that can lie on a single line (sunny). Since S is a triangular set, we can consider lines that intersect S in many points. For a line L, the intersection L в€© S is a set of lattice points. The maximum size of such a set depends on the slope. For slopes 0, в€ћ, -1, we get many points (like rows, columns, diagonals). For other slopes, the number of lattice points on a line is limited, but they could still be many if the line passes through many lattice points (like with rational slope). For example, line y = (1/2)x + 1/2 passes through (1,1) and (3,2) and (5,3) etc. So for n large, a line with slope 1/2 can pass through many points in S if S includes points with coordinates that satisfy that equation. But S is bounded, so the number of points on a line is at most about n (if slope is rational with small denominator). Actually for a line with rational slope p/q in lowest terms, the set of integer points on it is an arithmetic progression with common difference (q,p) in terms of (x,y). The number of integer points within the triangle could be about n/ something. Let's examine.

Consider a line L: y = mx + c, with m rational, say m = p/q in lowest terms, and c rational such that the line passes through integer points. Then integer points (x,y) on L satisfy y = (p/q)x + c. Multiply: qy = px + qc. For integer x,y, the right side must be integer, so c must be such that qc is integer. So we can write c = d/q for some integer d. Then equation becomes qy = px + d. Then integer solutions correspond to x в‰Ў something mod q. So the set of integer points is an arithmetic progression with step (q,p) in (x,y). So the number of such points with x between 1 and n (or up to sum bound) is at most about n / something. But we have n lines, and total points N в‰€ n^2/2. So if each sunny line can cover at most about n points, then n lines could cover up to n^2 points, which is enough, but we need to cover exactly N в‰€ n^2/2 points, so it's plausible. However we need to see if we can cover all points with n sunny lines. The obstacle is that some points might be hard to cover with sunny lines because they are, for example, points that are "isolated" in the sense that any line containing them also contains some other points that are not in S, but that's okay. The difficulty is that to cover many points, lines with slopes 0,в€ћ,-1 are efficient, but they are not sunny. So if we restrict to sunny lines, we might need to use many lines to cover all points, possibly more than n. But we only have n lines. So the question reduces to: is the covering number (minimum number of lines needed to cover S) equal to n? Or is it less than n? Let's find the minimum number of lines required to cover all points in S (with lines possibly any). We can use, for example, all n horizontal lines. That's n lines. We need a systematic approach.

LetвЂ™s define:

S_n = {(a,b) в€€ в„•^+ Г— в„•^+ : a+b в‰¤ n+1}. (nв‰Ґ3). We have n lines. We want to know possible k.

We can think in terms of covering each point with a line. Since we have only n lines, we can try to maximize sunny lines, i.e., minimize non-sunny lines. The bound on k_max is n - t_min, where t_min is the minimum number of non-sunny lines needed to cover S_n. So we need to compute t_min.

Then we also need to determine which k в‰¤ k_max are achievable. Usually, if we have a covering with t non-sunny lines and n-t sunny lines, then we can increase t by adding more non-sunny lines (or using non-sunny lines that are not optimal). But to achieve a smaller k, we can simply take a covering with more non-sunny lines, e.g., use all n lines non-sunny, giving k=0. So any k from 0 up to k_max is achievable? Not necessarily, because there might be gaps: for some k < k_max, there might be no covering with exactly k sunny lines. However we can try to modify a covering with k_max sunny lines to reduce the number of sunny lines by replacing some sunny lines with non-sunny lines, but we must maintain the covering property. Usually, if we have a covering with t non-sunny lines and n-t sunny lines, then we can increase t (decrease sunny) by adding additional non-sunny lines (but we have a fixed number n of lines; we cannot add lines). So to reduce sunny count, we need to use more non-sunny lines. That is, we want a covering where some of the lines are non-sunny. If we have a covering with t_min non-sunny lines, then we can also have coverings with t > t_min non-sunny lines, because we can take a covering with t_min non-sunny lines and then replace some sunny lines with non-sunny lines (but we cannot add lines; we have n lines total. So if we want a covering with, say, t = t_min + 1 non-sunny lines, then we would have n - (t_min+1) sunny lines. But we need to see whether we can find a covering with that many non-sunny lines. Since we can always take a covering where all lines are non-sunny (t=n), that's the extreme. So for any t between t_min and n, we might be able to construct a covering with exactly t non-sunny lines. But we need to check if we can achieve t = t_min + 1, etc. Usually, we can take the covering with t_min non-sunny lines and then replace some sunny lines with non-sunny lines, but we must keep the covering property. However if we replace a sunny line with a non-sunny line, we might cover more points (since non-sunny can cover many points). That seems plausible: we can take the configuration with t_min non-sunny lines and n-t_min sunny lines, and then replace one sunny line with a non-sunny line that covers some points that were not covered by that sunny line, but we need to ensure that after replacement, all points are covered. Since we are adding a non-sunny line (which can cover many points) and removing a sunny line, the union may change. But we can choose the non-sunny line to be something like a row that covers a lot of points; we might need to adjust.

But perhaps a more straightforward approach: Show that for any t between t_min and n, there exists a covering with exactly t non-sunny lines (and n-t sunny) by taking a covering with t_min non-sunny lines and then adding additional non-sunny lines that are redundant (i.e., they cover points already covered). But we cannot add lines because we have fixed n lines. However we can replace some sunny lines with non-sunny lines, but then we have fewer sunny lines. To achieve a specific k (sunny count), we need to design a covering with that number. So we need to determine the achievable k values. Let's try to construct for various n.

First, note that we can always take all n lines to be horizontal lines (y = 1,2,...,n). That's a covering with k=0. Similarly, we can take n-1 horizontals (y=1,...,n-1) and one sunny line that covers the points with y=n (which are (1,n), (2,n-1), ..., (n,1) but only those with a+b в‰¤ n+1). Better approach: Use a combinatorial argument based on the three families. Let's denote:

Let A be the set of lines that are not sunny. They are of three types: horizontal (H), vertical (V), and diagonal (D) (with slope -1). Since they are distinct, we can count how many points from S lie on each line.

Consider the points (a,b) with a,bв‰Ґ1, a+b в‰¤ n+1. For each such point, it must be on some line. The lines are distinct. We can try to bound the number of points that can be covered by sunny lines. Since sunny lines cannot be horizontal, vertical, or slope -1. But they can be any other slope. So perhaps we can use a pigeonhole principle on the points with a certain property, like the set of points where a+b is constant (diagonals). Those are covered by diagonal lines (non-sunny) or by sunny lines (which could intersect them). But the diagonal lines are not sunny. However we might be able to show that if we have too many sunny lines, then some points cannot be covered without enough non-sunny lines. Let's attempt to derive a lower bound on t (number of non-sunny lines) based on n.

Observation: The points (1,1), (2,2), ..., (вЊЉ(n+1)/2вЊ‹, вЊЉ(n+1)/2вЊ‹) lie on the line y = x. That line is sunny. So if we want to cover these points, we could use that line. But we can also cover them using other lines. However, the number of points on a sunny line is limited. The maximum number of points from S that can lie on a sunny line is something like floor((n+1)/2). Let's compute that precisely.

Consider a line L with slope s not equal to 0, в€ћ, -1. For integer points (x,y) on L, the differences are integer. The line can be expressed as y = mx + c. For it to contain many integer points, m must be rational. The maximum number of points from S on a line is attained when the line is one of the "bad" directions (horizontal, vertical, slope -1). For sunny directions, the maximum is something like floor((n+1)/2) for slope 1. But we also have slope 1/2, etc. But maybe we can bound the total number of points that can be covered by a set of sunny lines, and then conclude that to cover all N points, we need at least something like вЊ€N / max_sunny_coverageвЊ‰ sunny lines? That would be an upper bound on k_max (since we have n lines total). But we are trying to find the maximum k. That's the number of sunny lines that can be used in a covering. If we have k sunny lines, they cover at most k * M points, where M is the maximum number of points from S that a sunny line can contain. The remaining points must be covered by the non-sunny lines. Since we have n - k non-sunny lines, they can cover at most (n - k) * n points (if each is of a type that covers n points). But overlaps may reduce. However we can try to bound the maximum possible number of points that can be covered by k sunny lines. But the covering condition is that the union of all lines covers S. So if we have k sunny lines, they cover some subset U. The rest of S (S \ U) must be covered by the non-sunny lines. Since non-sunny lines can cover at most n points each (but they can cover up to n points). So we need |S \ U| в‰¤ (n - k) * n. That's a necessary condition. But we can also use that the non-sunny lines can cover points that are also covered by sunny lines, so it's not a tight bound. But to maximize k, we want to minimize the number of non-sunny lines. If we have many sunny lines, then the number of non-sunny lines is small, and they must cover the points that the sunny lines miss. So we can try to find the largest k such that there exists a covering with k sunny lines. That's what we are doing.

But perhaps it's impossible because the maximum number of points that a sunny line can contain is 4, but to cover 28 points we would need 7 lines with no overlap. That would require a partition of S_7 into 7 subsets each of size 4. Is that possible? Let's examine the geometry. The points in S_7 are arranged in a triangular grid. The total number of points is 28. The maximum size of a set of collinear points from S_7 on a sunny line is 4. So to cover all 28 points with 7 sunny lines, we need each line to cover 4 points and they must be disjoint. This would be a covering of the triangle by 7 lines each with 4 points. However, note that lines are straight, and they cannot intersect (except at points that are not covered by multiple lines) but they could intersect at points that are on both lines; but if they intersect at a point, then that point would be covered by both lines, causing overlap. If we want disjointness, we need the lines to be such that no point lies on two of them. But they could still intersect (if they are not parallel) and intersect at a point; that point would be on both lines, causing overlap. To avoid overlap, we need the lines to be pairwise parallel (or disjoint). If they are not parallel, they intersect at some point. That point, if it belongs to S, would be covered by two sunny lines, reducing union size. So to achieve union of size N with 7 sunny lines, we need the sunny lines to be pairwise parallel (or at least no two intersect in S). But if they are parallel, they are distinct, and they partition S by the intercept. For a given slope, the lines of that slope with different intercepts are parallel. If we take, say, all lines with slope 1 (y = x + c), then they are parallel and they partition S by the difference b - a. So we can get a covering where each point belongs to exactly one such line. But the number of such lines needed to cover all points is the number of distinct differences, which is 2n-1. That's > n. So with n lines, we cannot cover all points with parallel lines of a single slope. However we could use different slopes, but then lines of different slopes intersect. That creates overlaps. Now, prove the upper bound: In any covering of S_n by n distinct lines, at most 3 lines are sunny.

Approach: Consider the points on the "hypotenuse" (x+y = n+1). There are n such points. They lie on a line L0 (slope -1). If a line is sunny, it cannot be parallel to L0, so it can intersect L0 at most once. Therefore, each sunny line can cover at most 1 point from the hypotenuse. Let s be the number of sunny lines. Then the total number of points from the hypotenuse covered by sunny lines is at most s. The remaining points from the hypotenuse (at least n - s) must be covered by non-sunny lines. But non-sunny lines are of three types. Among them, only those that are parallel to L0 (i.e., slope -1) can cover multiple points from the hypotenuse. However a non-sunny line of slope -1 can cover all points of the hypotenuse (if it's exactly L0). But there could be multiple such lines (different intercepts). The hypotenuse points are on a single line. Any line that is not L0 but has slope -1 will intersect L0 at at most one point, so can cover at most 1 point from the hypotenuse. So to cover many points from the hypotenuse, we need either the line L0 itself (or a line with slope -1 that is L0, which is unique) to cover all n points. So if we have the line L0 (non-sunny) as one of our n lines, then all hypotenuse points are covered by that line. That's good. If we don't have L0, then we need to cover the hypotenuse points using other lines. But each sunny line covers at most 1, and each non-sunny line that is not L0 (but slope -1) also covers at most 1. So we would need at least n lines to cover the n points. But we only have n lines total. So if we don't have L0, then to cover all n points of the hypotenuse we would need at least n lines, which is exactly n. That could be possible if each line covers exactly one point from the hypotenuse, but they also cover other points. However we also need to cover the interior points. So maybe it's possible to have n lines where none is L0, but each line covers one point from the hypotenuse, and the rest of the points are covered by those lines as well. But we also have the possibility of using lines that are not sunny (horizontal/vertical) which intersect the hypotenuse at one point, but can cover many other points. That seems plausible. But we need a bound on sunny lines. The key is that if we have many sunny lines, then the number of non-sunny lines is small, and they cannot cover the hypotenuse efficiently. But perhaps we can derive a contradiction with the total number of lines (n) and the number of points on the hypotenuse, using a counting argument that involves both sunny and non-sunny lines.

Let's denote the set H = {(a,b) в€€ S_n : a+b = n+1}. There are n points. Each line L (distinct) can intersect H in at most 1 point. So the total number of points from H covered by all n lines is at most n (since each line can cover at most one). That's trivial. But we need to cover H. So H must be covered by the n lines. Since each line can cover at most 1 point, we need at least n lines to cover all n points. Indeed, if each line covers at most one point from H, then to cover all n points we need at least n lines. Since we have exactly n lines, it's possible only if each line covers exactly one point from H, and no line covers more than one. So for a covering of S_n with n lines, it must be that each line covers at most one point from H, and the collection of lines covers all H. Therefore, for each point in H, there is a unique line among the n lines that passes through it (if two lines passed through the same point, that's fine; but they'd cover that point, but then they'd be covering that point; but the condition "at most one" is about intersection with H. A line could intersect H at one point. If two lines intersect H at the same point, that's still one point covered by two lines. That's okay. But then the bound on number of lines needed to cover H is not n because one line can cover multiple points, but a line can intersect H at most once. So the maximum number of points from H that a single line can cover is 1. Therefore, to cover all n points, we need at least n lines. Since we have n lines, it's necessary that each line covers exactly one point from H and that no point is covered by two lines (or some points covered by multiple lines but still total lines n could cover H if some lines cover more than one? But they cannot cover more than one because intersection with H is at most one. So if a line covers 1 point, it's okay. If a line covers 0 points, then the remaining lines must cover all n points, but they can cover at most 1 each. So we would need at least n lines. Since we have n lines, it's necessary that each line covers exactly one point from H, and that those points are distinct. But could it be that some lines cover 0 points, and some lines cover 1 point, but we still have n lines total; if there are fewer than n lines covering H, then the lines covering H must be at least n. Since we have n lines, it's possible that some lines cover 0 points, and the others cover 1 point each, but then the number of lines covering H is less than n, so they can cover at most that many points. For n points, we need at least n lines covering H. So we need exactly n lines covering H (each covering one point), and the remaining lines (which are all n) cover H; if there are any lines that cover 0 points, then we need more lines to cover H. So indeed, for a covering of S_n with n lines, it must be that each line covers at least one point from H, and since each line can cover at most one, we have that each line covers exactly one point from H, and all these points are distinct. Therefore, the n lines are in bijection with the n points of H. So we can conclude: The n lines, when restricted to H, partition H (each point lies on exactly one line, and each line contains exactly one point of H). That's a strong condition.

Now, we also have that H is the set of points on the line x+y = n+1. So each line L_i passes through exactly one point of H. Moreover, L_i is not required to be of a certain type; it could be sunny or non-sunny. But if L_i is sunny, it's not parallel to the line x+y = n+1, so it can intersect H at that point. That's fine. So we have n lines, each intersecting H in a unique point. That's consistent.

Now, the problem asks to determine all k such that there exist n distinct lines satisfying both conditions. The covering condition is that every point in S lies on at least one line. The key seems to be that if we have many sunny lines, then the non-sunny lines are few, and they must cover many points. But the non-sunny lines are limited in type. Thus the argument must be more subtle: it's not about coverage size, but about the fact that the sunny lines are not allowed to be parallel to the three forbidden directions, so they cannot cover the points that lie on those lines efficiently, but we can cover them with non-sunny lines. Thus we need a more geometric argument.

Maybe we can prove that in any covering with n lines, the number of sunny lines is at most 3 by considering the three families of lines (rows, columns, diagonals) and using the pigeonhole principle on the points that are not on any of the non-sunny lines. The coverage bound we used (n - v) is an upper bound on the number of points covered by non-vertical lines, but we also have vertical lines covering points. So the total coverage is at most (n - v) + (v * n) (since each vertical line covers up to n points). But we need to cover n points. That's trivial. The earlier reasoning that v must be 0 is flawed because it assumed that no line covers multiple points, but we can have a line that covers all n points. The argument we gave about "maximum coverage of left axis by lines that are not vertical is (n - v)" is correct, but we also have vertical lines. So the total coverage is not simply (n - v). It's (n - v) + (v * n) (if all vertical lines are x=1). But are we allowed to have multiple vertical lines? They would be distinct lines, but all have the same x-coordinate (since they are vertical). They could be x = 1, x = 2, etc. But the left axis is x=1. So only the line x=1 can cover points on that axis. Other vertical lines (x=2) would intersect x=1 at no points (they are parallel and distinct). So they contribute nothing to covering the left axis. So to cover the left axis efficiently, we would include the line x=1. That's one vertical line. That line covers all n points. So we can have v = 1. That's okay. But the argument that v must be 0 used the idea that the maximum coverage from non-vertical lines is n - v, and we need n points covered; but that's not a contradiction because the vertical line covers many points. So we cannot conclude v = 0. Let's re-evaluate:

We have n lines L_i. For each L_i, the intersection with the line x = 1 (the left axis) is at most 1 point. So the number of points on the left axis that are covered by the union of all L_i is at most (n) because each L_i contributes at most 1 distinct point (they could be the same point, but then union size is в‰¤ n). However that's an upper bound on the size of the union of points on that axis that are covered by the lines. To cover all n points on that axis, we need that the union has size n. That is possible if each line covers a distinct point. But if any line is x = 1, then that line covers all n points, so the union size is n (since all points are covered). So that's also fine. So the earlier reasoning that v must be 0 is invalid. The mistake was: we considered the bound (n - v) as the maximum number of points that can be covered by non-vertical lines, but we also have vertical lines, but they can cover up to n points each. However the bound (n - v) is about points covered by non-vertical lines, but the vertical lines also cover points. The total covered is (n - v) + (v * n) (if each vertical line is x=1, but if they are not x=1, they cover none). But we have only one vertical line that can be x=1, so v is at most 1. The bound (n - v) is an upper bound on the number of points on the left axis that are covered by non-vertical lines. The vertical line x=1 covers n points. So the total covered is at most n (from vertical) + (n - v) from non-vertical, but those could overlap. But the bound we need is that we need to cover all n points. With v=1, we have n points covered by the vertical line, so the axis is covered regardless of the other lines. So that's okay. So no contradiction.

Thus the axes coverage does not force any restrictions on the number of sunny lines. So that argument is not sufficient.

We need a different approach. Let's think about the problem more systematically. It might be that the answer is indeed {0,1,3} for all n в‰Ґ 3. We have constructions for 0,1,3. For 2, we need to prove impossibility. So we need a proof that if there exist n distinct lines covering S_n, then the number of sunny lines is not 2. That is a necessary condition. We can try to prove that in any covering, the number of lines that are not horizontal, not vertical, not slope -1 is either 0,1,3. But we need a proof.

Maybe we can use a parity or counting argument involving the slopes of the lines and the points. Let's attempt a more combinatorial approach.

Consider the set of points S_n. Each point has integer coordinates. Define a function f(a,b) = a+b. The points with a+b = d lie on a line of slope -1. There are n such points. The horizontal lines are of the form y = c. The vertical lines are of the form x = a. The diagonal lines are of the form x+y = d. Let's denote:

- H: set of horizontal non-sunny lines (c).
- V: set of vertical non-sunny lines (a).
- D: set of diagonal non-sunny lines (d).

Now, the union of these lines covers all points. We want to show that s в‰¤ 3. Actually we need to show that s cannot be 2. But maybe we can prove a stronger statement: s can be 0,1, or 3 only. Assume s в‰Ґ 4. Then we have at least 4 sunny lines. Consider the points on the "hypotenuse" H = { (a,b) в€€ S_n : a+b = n+1 }. There are n such points. Each sunny line can intersect H at most once. So at most s points on H are covered by sunny lines. The remaining n - s points must be covered by non-sunny lines. The non-sunny lines are of three types. Among them, only those that are diagonal (slope -1) can cover multiple points on H. A diagonal line with equation x+y = d covers points with sum d. If d = n+1, it covers all n points on H. If d в‰  n+1, it covers at most 1 point on H (since it's parallel and distinct). So to cover the n - s points, we could either have the line x+y = n+1 as a non-sunny line (which covers all), or else we need enough non-sunny lines that are diagonal (or other) to cover those points. But we have n - s non-sunny lines. Among them, some could be diagonal. The number of diagonal lines we have is at most n - s. The maximum number of points on H that can be covered by non-sunny lines (excluding the line H itself) is at most (n - s) (if each such line is diagonal but not H, they each cover at most 1 point). If we have the line H itself, then it covers all n points, and we can ignore the rest. So to cover the n - s points, we could either have H as a line, then we are done; or else we need at least n - s diagonal lines (but we have n - s lines total, so they'd all be diagonal, each covering a distinct point). Together with s from sunny lines, we have в‰¤ n. That's okay, but we need to cover n points. The only way to cover all n points with n lines is if each line covers exactly one point on H and no line covers more than one. That would be possible if we have n lines, each covering a distinct point on H. That would give coverage of n points. So we could have s sunny lines covering s points, and the remaining n-s non-sunny lines covering the other n-s points. That would be a covering of H. So H does not force any contradiction. So again not enough.

But we need to derive that if there are exactly 2 sunny lines, then some point cannot be covered. Let's attempt to search for a proof.

Another idea: Use the concept of "covering number" with respect to the three families. Maybe we need to consider the points with a+b = 2,3,...,n+1 and the points with a=1, b=1, etc., and use a double counting of incidences between lines and points. Thus we need a different angle.

So if we don't have that line, we would need at least n - s non-sunny lines to cover those points. But we have only n - s non-sunny lines total. So it's possible that we have that vertical line. So that doesn't give a contradiction.

Alternatively, consider the points with a = b (main diagonal). There are about n/2 points. A sunny line can intersect that diagonal in at most 1 point. So at most s points on that diagonal can be covered by sunny lines. The rest must be covered by non-sunny lines. The only non-sunny lines that can cover points on that diagonal are those that are not parallel to that diagonal? Actually a non-sunny line of type H (horizontal) or V (vertical) can intersect the diagonal at most once. The diagonal is a line of slope 1. A line with slope -1 (diagonal) can intersect the diagonal at one point. So to cover many points on the diagonal, we need many lines. That seems messy.

So we can try to cover S with sunny lines that are not axis-aligned. However we need to cover all points. Since there are many points, we may need many lines. The total number of lines is n. Now we need to generalize for n>=3. The problem says "Determine all nonnegative integers k such that there exist n distinct lines in the plane satisfying both of the following: ..." We have S of size N = n(n+1)/2. We have n lines. The lines are distinct. The condition about sunny lines: a line is sunny if it is not parallel to x-axis, y-axis, or line x+y=0. Let's analyze the covering constraints more deeply. The points are lattice points in a triangle. We want to cover them with n lines. The lines are distinct. The condition "exactly k of the n lines are sunny" means we can choose any set of lines with that property.

We need to find all k for which there exists a set of n distinct lines covering S, and exactly k are sunny.

Observations:

- If we use a non-sunny line (horizontal, vertical, or slope -1), it is very effective at covering many points (like a row, column, or diagonal). So to minimize the number of sunny lines, we can use many non-sunny lines. But we want to maximize sunny lines (k). The maximum possible sunny lines is n (all sunny). So we need to determine whether we can cover S with n sunny lines. If yes, then k=n is achievable. If not, then the maximum achievable k is something less. We also need to see if intermediate k are achievable.

Let's test n=4 with an attempt to find 4 sunny lines covering all points. Maybe we can extend the n=3 construction. In n=3, we had points (1,1),(1,2),(1,3),(2,1),(2,2),(3,1). We used lines:

- L1: through (1,1) and (2,2) (slope 1)
- L2: through (2,1) and (1,3) (slope -2)
- L3: through (1,2) and (3,1) (slope -1/2)

L1 covers (1,1),(2,2). L2 covers (2,1),(1,3). L3 covers (1,2),(3,1). So each line covers two points, and the three lines cover all six points.

Thus with 4 lines, we cannot cover all points if we restrict to sunny lines. But perhaps we can design a different set of 4 sunny lines covering all 10 points. Let's attempt a systematic approach.

We need to cover S, which is a set of integer points in a triangle. The lines are not required to be distinct in terms of slope or type, but they must be distinct lines. We can choose any lines. For covering with all sunny lines, we can think about using lines that are not parallel to the three forbidden directions. The set of points S has some structure. Perhaps we can cover S with n lines that are all of the form y = mx + b with m being a rational number where denominator is something, and intercepts chosen so that each line covers a "row" or "column" of points but not exactly; we can use lines with slope 1 that cover the diagonal points, and then use lines with slope 2 that cover other points, etc. But we need to check feasibility.

Alternate viewpoint: The condition that a line is sunny is that its slope is not 0, в€ћ, or -1. So we can have lines with slope 1, 2, -2, 1/2, etc. So maybe we can cover S with n sunny lines by taking, for each b from 1 to n, a line that passes through all points with y-coordinate b (i.e., horizontal) is not sunny. So we cannot use that. But we could use lines that are nearly horizontal but with a small slope, but they won't cover many points because the points are on integer lattice, and a line with slope not 0 will intersect each horizontal line at most once. So to cover many points, it's more efficient to have lines that are axis-aligned or with slope -1. So perhaps for n>3, it's impossible to cover S with n sunny lines. Let's test n=4 with a computer-like mental search. But we can reason combinatorially.

We have n lines. The total number of points is N = n(n+1)/2. If each line covers at most some number of points, then with n lines we need to cover N points. But we can have overlap. However, if we want to cover all points with sunny lines, each line can contain at most some number of points from S. Let's find an upper bound on the number of points from S that can lie on a single line (sunny). Since S is a triangular set, we can consider lines that intersect S in many points. For a line L, the intersection L в€© S is a set of lattice points. The maximum size of such a set depends on the slope. For slopes 0, в€ћ, -1, we get many points (like rows, columns, diagonals). For other slopes, the number of lattice points on a line is limited, but they could still be many if the line passes through many lattice points (like with rational slope). For example, line y = (1/2)x + 1/2 passes through (1,1) and (3,2) and (5,3) etc. So for n large, a line with slope 1/2 can pass through many points in S if S includes points with coordinates that satisfy that equation. But S is bounded, so the number of points on a line is at most about n (if slope is rational with small denominator). Actually for a line with rational slope p/q in lowest terms, the set of integer points on it is an arithmetic progression with common difference (q,p) in terms of (x,y). The number of integer points within the triangle could be about n/ something. Let's examine.

Consider a line L: y = mx + c, with m rational, say m = p/q in lowest terms, and c rational such that the line passes through integer points. Then integer points (x,y) on L satisfy y = (p/q)x + c. Multiply: qy = px + qc. For integer x,y, the right side must be integer, so c must be such that qc is integer. So we can write c = d/q for some integer d. Then equation becomes qy = px + d. Then integer solutions correspond to x в‰Ў something mod q. So the set of integer points is an arithmetic progression with step (q,p) in (x,y). So the number of such points with x between 1 and n (or up to sum bound) is at most about n / something. But we have n lines, and total points N в‰€ n^2/2. So if each sunny line can cover at most about n points, then n lines could cover up to n^2 points, which is enough, but we need to cover exactly N в‰€ n^2/2 points, so it's plausible. However we need to see if we can cover all points with n sunny lines. The obstacle is that some points might be hard to cover with sunny lines because they are, for example, points that are "isolated" in the sense that any line containing them also contains some other points that are not in S, but that's okay. The difficulty is that to cover many points, lines with slopes 0,в€ћ,-1 are efficient, but they are not sunny. So if we restrict to sunny lines, we might need to use many lines to cover all points, possibly more than n. But we only have n lines. So the question reduces to: is the covering number (minimum number of lines needed to cover S) equal to n? Or is it less than n? Let's find the minimum number of lines required to cover all points in S (with lines possibly any). We can use, for example, all n horizontal lines. That's n lines. Similarly, all n vertical lines, or all n diagonals (x+y constant). So the covering number is at most n. Could it be less than n? For n=3, we found a covering with 3 lines (sunnies). That's n. Could we cover with 2 lines? That seems impossible because the points are many and not all collinear. So the minimum number is n. Indeed, S has points with distinct y-values, so to cover all points we need at least n lines if each line can cover at most one point in each row? But a line can cover multiple points with same y only if it's horizontal. So to cover all points, if we use only sunny lines, we might need more than n lines? But we already covered with 3 sunny lines for n=3. For n=4, we attempted but seemed not to cover all with 4 sunny lines. Let's try more systematically.

We need to determine all k such that there exists a configuration. This is a combinatorial geometry problem; we need to derive necessary conditions and then show sufficiency by constructing examples. The problem likely expects an answer like: k can be any integer from 0 to n, except that if n is even, then k cannot be n-1? But we saw n=4 (even) and k=3 (n-1) is possible. So that's not it. Maybe the pattern is: k can be any integer except n-2? That seems unlikely.

Let's attempt to derive a lower bound on the number of non-sunny lines required to cover S. Suppose we have a covering of S with n lines. Consider the points (1,1), (2,2), ..., (m,m) where m is the maximum integer such that 2m в‰¤ n+1. That's about floor((n+1)/2). These points lie on the line y = x (slope 1). That line is sunny. So if we want to cover these points with sunny lines, we could use that line. But we can also cover them with non-sunny lines. However the difficulty is to cover points that are not on any sunny line? Actually all points in S can be covered by sunny lines if we have enough of them, but we only have n lines. The question is whether it's possible to cover S with n sunny lines. So we need to check if the covering number (minimum number of lines to cover S) is n, and whether that minimum can be achieved with sunny lines. For n=3, we found a covering with 3 sunny lines, so the minimum is в‰¤3. Could we cover with 2 lines? That seems impossible. So the minimum is 3. So for n=3, the covering number is n. For n=4, the covering number is at most 4 (since we have a covering with 4 lines, e.g., all horizontals). But is it possible to cover with 3 lines? Let's test: can we cover S (10 points) with 3 lines? Probably not. So the covering number is 4. But we found a covering with 4 lines that is not all sunny (we used 1 non-sunny). We need a systematic approach.

LetвЂ™s define:

S_n = {(a,b) в€€ в„•^+ Г— в„•^+ : a+b в‰¤ n+1}. (nв‰Ґ3). We have n lines. We want to know possible k.

We can think in terms of covering each point with a line. Since we have only n lines, we can try to maximize sunny lines, i.e., minimize non-sunny lines. The bound on k_max is n - t_min, where t_min is the minimum number of non-sunny lines needed to cover S_n. So we need to compute t_min.

Then we also need to determine which k в‰¤ k_max are achievable. Usually, if we have a covering with t non-sunny lines and n-t sunny lines, then we can increase t by adding more non-sunny lines (or using non-sunny lines that are not optimal). But to achieve a smaller k, we can simply take a covering with more non-sunny lines, e.g., use all n lines non-sunny, giving k=0. So any k from 0 up to k_max is achievable? Not necessarily, because there might be gaps: for some k < k_max, there might be no covering with exactly k sunny lines. However we can try to modify a covering with k_max sunny lines to reduce the number of sunny lines by replacing some sunny lines with non-sunny lines, but we must maintain the covering property. Usually, if we have a covering with t non-sunny lines and n-t sunny lines, then we can increase t (decrease sunny) by adding additional non-sunny lines (but we have a fixed number n of lines; we cannot add lines). So to reduce sunny count, we need to use more non-sunny lines. That is, we want a covering where some of the lines are non-sunny. If we have a covering with t_min non-sunny lines, then we can also have coverings with t > t_min non-sunny lines, because we can take a covering with t_min non-sunny lines and then replace some sunny lines with non-sunny lines (but we cannot add lines; we have n lines total. So if we want a covering with, say, t = t_min + 1 non-sunny lines, then we would have n - (t_min+1) sunny lines. But we need to see whether we can find a covering with that many non-sunny lines. Since we can always take a covering where all lines are non-sunny (t=n), that's the extreme. So for any t between t_min and n, we might be able to construct a covering with exactly t non-sunny lines. But we need to check if we can achieve t = t_min + 1, etc. Usually, we can take the covering with t_min non-sunny lines and then replace some sunny lines with non-sunny lines, but we must keep the covering property. However if we replace a sunny line with a non-sunny line, we might cover more points (since non-sunny can cover many points). That seems plausible: we can take the configuration with t_min non-sunny lines and n-t_min sunny lines, and then replace one sunny line with a non-sunny line that covers some points that were not covered by that sunny line, but we need to ensure that after replacement, all points are covered. Since we are adding a non-sunny line (which can cover many points) and removing a sunny line, the union may change. But we can choose the non-sunny line to be something like a row that covers a lot of points; we might need to adjust.

But perhaps a more straightforward approach: Show that for any t between t_min and n, there exists a covering with exactly t non-sunny lines (and n-t sunny) by taking a covering with t_min non-sunny lines and then adding additional non-sunny lines that are redundant (i.e., they cover points already covered). But we cannot add lines because we have fixed n lines. However we can replace some sunny lines with non-sunny lines, but then we have fewer sunny lines. To achieve a specific k (sunny count), we need to design a covering with that number. So we need to determine the achievable k values. Let's try to construct for various n.

We need to find the minimum number of non-sunny lines required to cover S_n. This is similar to a covering problem where we can choose any lines, but non-sunny lines are limited in direction. However we can also use sunny lines, which we can design arbitrarily. So we want to minimize t, the number of non-sunny lines, while still being able to cover all points with n lines total. Since we have n lines total, we can use a certain number of sunny lines to cover the points that are not covered by the non-sunny lines. So we need to find the smallest t such that there exists a set of t non-sunny lines (each from the three families) and (n-t) sunny lines (any slopes not forbidden) whose union covers S_n. We can choose the sunny lines after selecting the non-sunny lines.

Thus t_min is the solution to an optimization problem. We can attempt to find a lower bound on t. Then construct examples achieving that bound. Then for each k в‰¤ n - t_min, we can try to achieve by using more non-sunny lines. But we also need to consider whether some intermediate k are impossible due to parity or structural constraints. For example, for n=5, k=2 is impossible. That's less than k_max=3. So there is a gap. So we need to determine all achievable k for each n.

Let's compute t_min for small n:

n=3: t_min = 0 (since 3 sunny lines work). So k_max = 3. Achievable k: 0,1,3. (k=2 impossible). So the set of k is {0,1,3}. That's not an interval. For n=4: t_min = 1 (since 4 sunny impossible). Now, prove the upper bound: In any covering of S_n by n distinct lines, at most 3 lines are sunny.

Approach: Consider the points on the "hypotenuse" (x+y = n+1). There are n such points. They lie on a line L0 (slope -1). If a line is sunny, it cannot be parallel to L0, so it can intersect L0 at most once. Therefore, each sunny line can cover at most 1 point from the hypotenuse. Let s be the number of sunny lines. The hypotenuse points are on a single line. Any line that is not L0 but has slope -1 will intersect L0 at at most one point, so can cover at most 1 point from the hypotenuse. So to cover many points from the hypotenuse, we need either the line L0 itself (or a line with slope -1 that is L0, which is unique) to cover all n points. So if we have the line L0 (non-sunny) as one of our n lines, then all hypotenuse points are covered by that line. That's good. If we don't have L0, then we need to cover the hypotenuse points using other lines. But each sunny line covers at most 1, and each non-sunny line that is not L0 (but slope -1) also covers at most 1. So we would need at least n lines to cover the n points. But we only have n lines total. So if we don't have L0, then to cover all n points of the hypotenuse we would need at least n lines, which is exactly n. So maybe it's possible to have n lines where none is L0, but each line covers one point from the hypotenuse, and the rest of the points are covered by those lines as well. But perhaps we can derive a contradiction with the total number of lines (n) and the number of points on the hypotenuse, using a counting argument that involves both sunny and non-sunny lines.

Now, note that H lies on the line L0: x+y = n+1. So each line L_i passes through a point of L0. So L_i is a line that meets L0 at that point. If L_i is sunny, it cannot be parallel to L0, but that's fine. So all lines intersect L0.

Now, the problem reduces to: For nв‰Ґ3, what are the possible numbers of sunny lines among n lines that satisfy:

- Each line intersects L0 in a unique point, and those points are the n points of H (the lattice points on L0 within S).

Now, we want to bound k (sunny lines). We can try to prove that at most 3 lines can be sunny. How to prove that? Suppose we have at least 4 sunny lines. Alternatively, consider the following: For each sunny line L, consider the set of points from S that lie on L. Since L is not horizontal, vertical, or slope -1, it has some slope. We might try to show that if there are many sunny lines, then some points from S cannot be covered because the lines would be forced to be in certain directions, leading to a contradiction with the covering of the boundary. But we need a rigorous proof.

Let's try a different perspective. Use the concept of "covering number" with respect to lines of certain slopes. The problem is essentially about covering a triangular grid with lines. The three forbidden slopes are those that can cover many points efficiently. The sunny lines are the complement. To cover S with n lines, we need to have enough lines of the forbidden directions to cover the "dense" parts. The covering with rows only (n-3 rows) and then sunny lines covering the leftover triangle of size 3 works, and in that covering, H is covered by sunny lines (since the leftover triangle includes points on H). Thus we need a different argument.

Now, the general proof for nв‰Ґ3: Suppose we have n lines covering S_n, with exactly 2 sunny lines. Then we need to show that it's impossible. We can try to prove that in any covering with n lines, the number of sunny lines is either 0,1, or 3. But we need a rigorous proof. Let's attempt to derive a contradiction using the points on the "axes" and the hypotenuse. Consider the set of points with a = 1 (vertical). There are n such points. And for points on the hypotenuse (a+b = n+1). Each of these three boundaries has size n. The non-sunny lines are of three types, each of which can cover many points on some boundaries. For example, a vertical line (x=1) covers all points on the left boundary. A horizontal line (y=1) covers all points on the bottom boundary. A diagonal line (x+y = constant) covers points on that diagonal. If we have many sunny lines, they cannot be those types. So the total coverage from left boundary by all lines is at most (n-2) from non-sunny lines (if they are all vertical or cover distinct points) plus 2 from sunny lines. But that's at most n, so it's possible to cover all n points. However we need to ensure that the non-sunny lines actually intersect the left boundary in distinct points. If we have a vertical line, it covers all. So that's efficient. If we don't have that line, then each non-sunny line can cover at most 1 point on that boundary. So to cover n points, we would need at least n non-sunny lines (if no sunny lines). But we have n-2 non-sunny lines, and 2 sunny lines, so the maximum number of points from left boundary that can be covered by sunny lines is 2. So total coverage from left boundary is at most (n-2) + 2 = n. So it's possible to cover all n points if the non-sunny lines intersect the left boundary in distinct points and cover n-2 points, and the sunny lines cover the remaining 2 points. That's plausible. So not contradictory.

Now, consider the points on the hypotenuse H. Size n. Sunny lines cover at most 2 points. Non-sunny lines cover the rest. So if all non-sunny lines are not slope -1, they can cover at most n-2 points from H. Together with 2 sunny points, we cover n points. That's possible. So again not contradictory.

Thus we need a more subtle argument. Perhaps we can consider the points where a = 1 and b = 1 (the corner). That's one point. Thus we need a more geometric argument.

Thus the axes coverage does not force any restrictions on the number of sunny lines. So that argument is not sufficient.

We need a different approach. Let's think about the problem more systematically. It might be that the answer is indeed {0,1,3} for all n в‰Ґ 3. We have constructions for 0,1,3. For 2, we need to prove impossibility. So we need a proof that if there exist n distinct lines covering S_n, then the number of sunny lines is not 2. That is a necessary condition. We can try to prove that in any covering, the number of lines that are not horizontal, not vertical, not slope -1 is either 0,1,3. But we need a proof.

Maybe we can use a parity or counting argument involving the slopes of the lines and the points. Let's attempt a more combinatorial approach.

Consider the set of points S_n. Each point has integer coordinates. Define a function f(a,b) = a+b. The points with a+b = d lie on a line of slope -1. There are n such points. Now, suppose we have n lines. Among them, let s be sunny. Then there are n-s non-sunny lines. The non-sunny lines are either horizontal, vertical, or diagonal (slope -1). The horizontal lines are of the form y = c. The vertical lines are of the form x = a. The diagonal lines are of the form x+y = d. Let's denote:

- H: set of horizontal non-sunny lines (c).
- V: set of vertical non-sunny lines (a).
- D: set of diagonal non-sunny lines (d).

Assume s в‰Ґ 4. Then we have at least 4 sunny lines. Consider the points on the "hypotenuse" H = { (a,b) в€€ S_n : a+b = n+1 }. There are n such points. Each sunny line can intersect H at most once. So at most s points on H are covered by sunny lines. The remaining n - s points must be covered by non-sunny lines. The non-sunny lines are of three types. Among them, only those that are diagonal (slope -1) can cover multiple points on H. A diagonal line with equation x+y = d covers points with sum d. If d = n+1, it covers all n points on H. If d в‰  n+1, it covers at most 1 point on H (since it's parallel and distinct). So to cover the n - s points, we could either have the line x+y = n+1 as a non-sunny line (which covers all), or else we need enough non-sunny lines that are diagonal (or other) to cover those points. But we have n - s non-sunny lines. Among them, some could be diagonal. The number of diagonal lines we have is at most n - s. The maximum number of points on H that can be covered by non-sunny lines (excluding the line H itself) is at most (n - s) (if each such line is diagonal but not H, they each cover at most 1 point). If we have the line H itself, then it covers all n points, and we can ignore the rest. So to cover the n - s points, we could either have H as a line, then we are done; or else we need at least n - s diagonal lines (but we have n - s lines total, so they'd all be diagonal, each covering a distinct point).
