Together with s from sunny lines, we have в‰¤ n. That's okay, but we need to cover n points. The only way to cover all n points with n lines is if each line covers exactly one point on H and no line covers more than one. That would be possible if we have n lines, each covering a distinct point on H. That would give coverage of n points. So we could have s sunny lines covering s points, and the remaining n-s non-sunny lines covering the other n-s points. That would be a covering of H. So H does not force any contradiction. So again not enough.

However we need to be careful: The complement bound argument assumes that the non-sunny lines are rows, which is the best coverage. But we don't know that the covering uses rows as non-sunny lines; we are trying to prove that s в‰¤ 3. If s в‰Ґ 4, then the number of non-sunny lines is n - s в‰¤ n - 4. To cover S_n, we need that the union of the non-sunny lines covers at least N - (max possible coverage by sunny lines). But we don't have an upper bound on the coverage by sunny lines in terms of M. However we can argue about the points that are not on any non-sunny line. Those points must be covered by sunny lines. Therefore, the number of such points is at most s * M. So we need to find a lower bound on the number of points not covered by non-sunny lines, assuming that we have n - s non-sunny lines (of types H,V,D). That lower bound will be something like: the maximum coverage of (n - s) non-sunny lines is achieved by using rows, and the complement is a triangle of size s. So the complement size is at least N - max_coverage. Since max_coverage в‰¤ something, the complement size в‰Ґ something. That something is a function of n and s. If s в‰Ґ 4, then complement size в‰Ґ 10? But we need to compute the complement size when we have (n - s) non-sunny lines and we want to minimize the complement (to make it possible to cover with sunny lines). The complement size is minimized when the non-sunny lines cover as many points as possible. The maximum coverage by (n - s) non-sunny lines is achieved by using rows (or columns) because they cover many points. So the minimal possible complement size (over all choices of (n - s) lines from the families) is N - max_coverage. That is, if we have (n - s) non-sunny lines, they cannot cover more than that max coverage. Thus the points not on any non-sunny line are at least that complement size. So we have:

|S_n \ U_non-sunny| в‰Ґ N - max_coverage_{(n-s)}.

Now, the sunny lines must cover these points, so we need:

s * M в‰Ґ N - max_coverage_{(n-s)}.

But careful: The sunny lines are s in number, each can cover at most M points from S_n. However they could also cover points that are already covered by non-sunny lines; but we need to cover the complement, so the number of points in the complement is a lower bound on the union of the sunny lines. The sunny lines could cover points outside the complement, but that's fine. The union of sunny lines must include the complement. So the total size of the union of sunny lines is at least the complement size. That union is at most s * M (since each line has at most M points). Therefore, we have:

complement size в‰¤ s * M.

So if we can show that for s = 2, the complement size (minimal possible) is > 2 * M, then impossible. For s = 2, n - s = n - 2. The maximum coverage by n - 2 non-sunny lines is achieved by using rows covering N - 3? Let's compute general n.

We have N = n(n+1)/2. For a given number of non-sunny lines t = n - s, the maximum coverage they can achieve is achieved by taking the t rows with largest sizes (i.e., rows y = 1..t). That coverage is sum_{i=1}^{t} (n+1 - i) = t(n+1) - t(t+1)/2.

Thus the minimal possible complement size (points not covered by those t lines) is N - that sum. Compute:

N - coverage = [n(n+1)/2] - [t(n+1) - t(t+1)/2] = [n(n+1) - 2t(n+1) + t(t+1)] / 2 = [ (n+1)(n - 2t) + t(t+1) ] / 2.

But we can also compute the complement size directly as the points with y в‰Ґ t+1. That's a triangle of size n - t. The number of points in that triangle is (n - t)(n - t + 1)/2. Because it's the triangle of size (n - t) (since the maximum sum is n+1, the rows from (t+1) to n have sizes n - t, n - t - 1, ..., 1). So complement size = (n - t)(n - t + 1)/2.

Thus if we have t non-sunny rows (horizontal), the complement is a triangle of size n - t. That's correct. For t = n - s, complement size = (s)(s+1)/2.

Now, if we have t non-sunny lines that are not all rows, they might cover fewer points, making the complement larger. So the complement size is at least (s)(s+1)/2 (since rows give the minimum complement). So we have:

|S_n \ U_non-sunny| в‰Ґ (s)(s+1)/2.

Now, the sunny lines must cover these points. So we need:

s * M в‰Ґ (s)(s+1)/2.

But M = max_{sunny L} |L в€© S_n|. For a sunny line, the maximum number of points from S_n that lie on it is вЊЉ(n+1)/2вЊ‹. However note that the complement points are a triangle of size s. The sunny lines are not restricted to that triangle; they can cover points anywhere. But we need to cover the complement, which is a subset of S_n. The maximum size a sunny line can have within that triangle is something like min(вЊЉ(n+1)/2вЊ‹, something). But we are bounding the total union of sunny lines. The union could be larger than the complement. But the complement is a set of points that must be covered. So the total number of points covered by sunny lines is at least the complement size. The sum of sizes of sunny lines (i.e., sum over sunny lines of |L в€© S_n|) is at most s * M. However the union size could be less than that sum due to overlaps. But we need a lower bound on the union size. The union size is at least the complement size. So we have:

complement size в‰¤ union size в‰¤ sum of sizes (since union в‰¤ sum). Actually union в‰¤ sum, but union could be much smaller. The inequality we need is: complement size в‰¤ union size, and union size в‰¤ s * M. So complement size в‰¤ s * M. That's a necessary condition. So we need:

(s)(s+1)/2 в‰¤ s * M в†’ (s+1)/2 в‰¤ M в†’ M в‰Ґ (s+1)/2.

Now, M = floor((n+1)/2). So we need floor((n+1)/2) в‰Ґ (s+1)/2. That gives an upper bound on s. If s is large, then (s+1)/2 may exceed M. For s = 2, (2+1)/2 = 1.5 в‰¤ M (which is at least 2 for nв‰Ґ3). So condition holds. For s = 3, (3+1)/2 = 2 в‰¤ M (M в‰Ґ 2). For s = 4, (4+1)/2 = 2.5 в‰¤ M? For n=7, M=4, so 2.5 в‰¤ 4, condition holds. So that doesn't forbid s=4. But we also have that the complement points are a triangle of size s, but the sunny lines may not be able to cover that triangle because the maximum per sunny line in that triangle is limited. However we only used the sum bound. The sum bound is an upper bound on the total number of incidences. But the union size could be less than that sum. The complement size is a lower bound on the union size. So we have:

complement size в‰¤ union size в‰¤ sum of sizes (since union в‰¤ sum). Actually union в‰¤ sum, but union could be smaller than sum. However we need an upper bound on union size, which is в‰¤ sum of sizes. The sum of sizes is в‰¤ s * M. So union в‰¤ s * M. That gives an inequality complement в‰¤ s * M. That is not contradictory for s=4. But we also have that the complement size is (s)(s+1)/2. So we need (s)(s+1)/2 в‰¤ s * M в†’ (s+1)/2 в‰¤ M. That's satisfied. So the necessary condition doesn't forbid s=4. However we also need that the sunny lines can cover the complement. That requires that the complement points are covered by the union of the sunny lines. Since the complement is a triangle of size s, we need to see if there exists a set of s sunny lines whose union contains that triangle. That's a stronger condition than just the size inequality. The size inequality only gives a bound on the total number of points that can be covered (if there is no overlap), but overlaps reduce union size. So to cover a triangle of size s, we need that the union size is at least that triangle's size. The sum of sizes could be large, but if the lines overlap heavily, the union could be small. The necessary condition for existence is that there is a covering of that triangle by s lines. That's a combinatorial geometry problem. For s=5, we attempted to see if there are 5 lines each covering 3 points, disjoint. That would require that the triangle of size 5 can be partitioned into 5 lines of 3 points each. ## Solution
We need to determine all nonnegative integers \(k\) such that there exist \(n\) distinct lines in the plane satisfying:
- For all positive integers \(a,b\) with \(a+b\le n+1\), the point \((a,b)\) lies on at least one of the lines;
- Exactly \(k\) of the \(n\) lines are **sunny** (i.e., not parallel to the \(x\)-axis, the \(y\)-axis, or the line \(x+y=0\)).

Let \(S_n=\{(a,b)\in\mathbb N^+\times\mathbb N^+\mid a+b\le n+1\}\).  
We will prove that the possible values of \(k\) are \(0,1,3\) for all \(n\ge 3\).

---

### 1.  Constructing configurations with \(k=0,1,3\)

**\(k=0\)** вЂ“ take the \(n\) horizontal lines \(y=1,2,\dots,n\).  
For any \((a,b)\in S_n\) we have \(b\le n\) and \(a\le n+1-b\), so the point lies on the line \(y=b\).  
All lines are horizontal, hence not sunny (parallel to \(x\)-axis).  
Thus \(k=0\) is achievable.

**\(k=1\)** вЂ“ take the \(n-1\) horizontal lines \(y=1,2,\dots,n-1\) and the sunny line \(y=1\)? Actually we need exactly one sunny line, so we can take \(n-1\) horizontals (nonвЂ‘sunny) and one sunny line through the single point \((1,n)\) (the only point of \(S_n\) with \(b=n\)).  
The line through \((1,n)\) with slope \(2\) (e.g. \(y=2x-(n-1)\)) is sunny.  
All points of \(S_n\) with \(b\le n-1\) are covered by the horizontals; the point \((1,n)\) is covered by the sunny line.  
Hence \(k=1\) is achievable.

**\(k=3\)** вЂ“ we give an explicit construction for any \(n\ge 3\).  

Take the first \(n-3\) horizontal lines \(y=1,2,\dots,n-3\).  
These are nonвЂ‘sunny and together cover all points with \(b\le n-3\).  

The points that are **not** covered by these horizontals are those with \(b\ge n-2\):
\[
T_n=\{(a,b)\in S_n\mid b\ge n-2\}.
\]
\(T_n\) is a triangle of side \(3\) (size \(3\)):
\[
|T_n|=\frac{3\cdot4}{2}=6.
\]
We claim that \(T_n\) can be covered by three sunny lines.  

Indeed, consider the three lines:
\[
\ell_1:\ y=x,\qquad
\ell_2:\ y=-2x+(n+1),\qquad
\ell_3:\ y=-\frac12 x+\frac{n+1}{2}.
\]
(We will verify that \(\ell_2,\ell_3\) are wellвЂ‘defined and sunny.)  

For \(n\ge3\) we have \(n+1\ge4\) and \(\frac{n+1}{2}\ge2\).  
LetвЂ™s examine the points of \(T_n\).  
Write \(b\) for the second coordinate; the rows are:

- \(b=n-2\): \(a\) runs from \(1\) to \(3\) (since \(a\le n+1-(n-2)=3\)).
- \(b=n-1\): \(a\) runs from \(1\) to \(2\).
- \(b=n\): \(a=1\) (only).

Thus \(T_n\) consists of the six points:
\[
(1,n-2),\;(2,n-2),\;(3,n-2),\;(1,n-1),\;(2,n-1),\;(1,n).
\]

Now we check that each of these points lies on one of \(\ell_1,\ell_2,\ell_3\):

- \(\ell_1\) (\(y=x\)) passes through \((1,1),(2,2),(3,3)\).  For our points, the ones with equal coordinates are \((1,1)\)? But \(b\) is at least \(n-2\ge1\) but \(b\) is not equal to \(a\) for most points.  Actually we need to find which points of \(T_n\) satisfy \(y=x\).  In \(T_n\) the second coordinate is large, while the first is small; the only candidate is if \(a=b\).  For \(b=n-2\), the maximum \(a\) is \(3\), so \(a=b\) is impossible unless \(n-2\le3\) and \(a=n-2\), but then \(a\) would be at most \(3\).  For \(n=3\) we have \(b=1\) and \(a=1\) appears in \(T_3\)? Let's compute for \(n=3\), \(T_3\) has points with \(b\ge1\)? Actually \(n-2=1\), so \(b\ge1\) are all points.  That's the whole triangle of size 3.  Our construction for \(n=3\) is exactly the three sunny lines covering the whole triangle.  For \(n>3\), \(b\) is at least \(n-2\ge2\), while the first coordinate is at most \(3\); the equality \(a=b\) never occurs because \(b\) is large.  So \(\ell_1\) does **not** contain any point of \(T_n\) when \(n>3\).  But we still need to cover \(T_n\) with three sunny lines.  The construction for \(n=3\) is different (all sunny lines).  For \(n\ge4\), we need to adjust the lines.

We made a mistake: the complement after using horizontals \(y=1,\dots,n-3\) is not a triangle of side 3; it's a triangle of side \(n-(n-3)=3\)? Actually \(n-(n-3)=3\) is the size of the triangle (since the remaining rows are \(b=n-2,n-1,n\) with sizes \(3,2,1\)).  So that's a triangle of side 3.  But the points in that triangle are those with \(b\ge n-2\).  The coordinates are \((a,b)\) with \(b\ge n-2\) and \(a\le n+1-b\).  For \(n=4\), \(b\ge2\) в†’ points: (1,2),(2,2),(3,2) (since aв‰¤4-2=2? Actually n+1-b = 5-b. For b=2, aв‰¤3. So (1,2),(2,2),(3,2). b=3 в†’ aв‰¤2 в†’ (1,3),(2,3). b=4 в†’ aв‰¤1 в†’ (1,4). That's 6 points.  So for n=4, T_4 = { (1,2),(2,2),(3,2),(1,3),(2,3),(1,4) }.  For n=5, T_5 = { (1,3),(2,3),(3,3),(1,4),(2,4),(1,5) }.  So indeed the triangle is shifted.

Now, can we cover T_n with three sunny lines?  The lines we proposed are:

в„“1: y = x (slope 1)
в„“2: y = -2x + (n+1)   (slope -2)
в„“3: y = -1/2 x + (n+1)/2   (slope -1/2)

But for n>3, the points in T_n have b at least n-2, which is large.  For example, for n=4, T_4 points are (1,2),(2,2),(3,2),(1,3),(2,3),(1,4).  Does в„“1 pass through any? (1,1) not in T_4, (2,2) is in T_4, (3,3) not in T_4.  So в„“1 covers (2,2) only.  в„“2: y = -2x + 5, at x=2 gives y=1, not in T_4; at x=1 gives y=3 в†’ (1,3) is in T_4; at x=3 gives y=-1.  So covers (1,3).  в„“3: y = -1/2 x + 2.5, at x=1 gives y=2 в†’ (1,2) is in T_4; at x=2 gives y=2 в†’ (2,2) is in T_4? Actually (2,2) is in T_4, but we already have (2,2) covered by в„“1; at x=3 gives y=2 в†’ (3,2) is in T_4; at x=4 gives y=1.5.  So в„“3 covers (1,2),(2,2),(3,2).  That's all points of T_4? Let's check: T_4 points are (1,2),(2,2),(3,2),(1,3),(2,3),(1,4).  в„“3 covers (1,2),(2,2),(3,2).  в„“1 covers (2,2).  в„“2 covers (1,3).  So (1,4) is not covered.  So that fails.  We need to find three sunny lines that cover all six points of T_4.  Earlier we found a covering for n=4 with 2 rows and 3 sunny lines, but that was a different configuration.  The construction for k=3 should be explicit.  Let's find an explicit covering for any nв‰Ґ3.

Idea: Use the following three sunny lines:
L1: y = x
L2: y = -2x + (n+3)   (maybe)
L3: y = -1/2 x + (n+3)/2   (maybe)

But we need to check.  For n=4, we already have a covering with 3 sunny lines covering T_4?  Let's try to find one.

We want to cover the points:
(1,2),(2,2),(3,2),(1,3),(2,3),(1,4).

We can try L1: y = x (covers (2,2))
L2: y = -2x + 6? That gives (1,4),(2,2) covered, (3,0) not.  So covers (1,4),(2,2).  L3: y = -1/2 x + 3.5? That gives (1,3),(3,2) (since -1.5+3.5=2).  So covers (1,3),(3,2).  But we have (2,3) not covered.  So maybe L2: y = -2x + 5 covers (1,3),(2,1) not, (3,-1).  That's (1,3) only.  L3: y = -1/2 x + 2.5 covers (1,2),(3,1) not, (2,2) (2,2) already, (1,2) is okay, (2,2) okay, (3,2) okay.  That covers (1,2),(2,2),(3,2).  Then we need (2,3) and (1,4).  L1 covers (2,2).  So (2,3) is uncovered.  L2 covers (1,3).  So (2,3) remains.  L3 covers (3,2).  So (2,3) not covered.  We could use L2: y = -2x + 7? That would be (1,5),(2,3),(3,1) not in T_4.  So covers (2,3) only.  Then we have L3 covering (1,2),(2,2),(3,2).  L1 covering (2,2).  L2 covering (2,3).  So (1,4) is uncovered.  So need a line covering (1,4).  Could be y = 2x - 3? That's (1,-1) not.  y = x + 3? (1,4) but then (2,5) not.  So line y = x+3 is sunny, covers (1,4).  Then we have L1: y=x, L2: y=x+3, L3: y=-1/2 x + 2.5 (covers (1,2),(2,2),(3,2)).  Then (2,3) is uncovered.  So need a line covering (2,3).  That could be y = -2x + 7? That gives (1,5),(2,3),(3,1) -> covers (2,3).  So we have three lines: y=x, y=x+3, y=-2x+7.  But these are three lines, not four.  Let's test coverage:

- y=x covers (2,2)
- y=x+3 covers (1,4)
- y=-2x+7 covers (2,3)

What about (3,2)?  y=-2x+7 at x=3 gives y=1, not (3,2).  (1,2) at y=x+3 is (1,4), not (1,2).  So not covered.  So we need to cover all six points.  Perhaps we can use lines of slopes 1, -2, -1/2 after appropriate translation.  Let's compute the points of T_n in terms of shifted coordinates.  Define u = a, v = b - (n-2).  Then b = v + (n-2).  The condition a+b в‰¤ n+1 becomes u + v в‰¤ 3.  And uв‰Ґ1, vв‰Ґ0.  So the points (u,v) are the integer points in the triangle with vertices (1,0), (1,2), (3,0) (since uв‰¤3 and vв‰¤2).  That's a triangle of side 3.  So indeed T_n is an affine image of S_3.  Therefore, if we can cover S_3 (the triangle of size 3) with 3 sunny lines, then by applying the same affine transformation (translation + maybe scaling) we can cover T_n.  However, note that the affine transformation must preserve the property of being sunny?  If we apply a similarity (translation and rotation), slopes change.  But we are free to choose lines in the plane; we can simply use the three lines that are the images of the lines from the S_3 covering under the translation (and maybe rotation).  Since we only need to cover T_n, which is a triangle of side 3 but shifted, we can take the three lines from the n=3 case (which are sunny) and translate them appropriately.  Because the n=3 case lines are sunny, and translating them preserves slopes, so they remain sunny.  So we can use the same three lines (y = x, y = -2x + 5, y = -1/2 x + 2.5) but translated so that they cover T_n.  However, we need to ensure that the lines we use are distinct and that they cover the points of T_n.  Let's test with n=4: T_4 points are (1,2),(2,2),(3,2),(1,3),(2,3),(1,4).  The lines from n=3 case (with points (1,1),(1,2),(1,3),(2,1),(2,2),(3,1)) are:

L1: y = x (covers (1,1),(2,2),(3,3))
L2: y = -2x + 5 (covers (1,3),(2,1),(3,-1)) -> (1,3),(2,1)
L3: y = -1/2 x + 2.5 (covers (1,2),(3,1)) -> (1,2),(3,1)

Now, if we translate these lines by adding (0,1) (i.e., shift y-coordinate up by 1), we get:

L1': y = x + 1 (covers (1,2),(2,3),(3,4))
L2': y = -2x + 6 (covers (1,4),(2,2),(3,0)) -> (1,4),(2,2)
L3': y = -1/2 x + 3.5 (covers (1,3),(3,2)) -> (1,3),(3,2)

Now, check coverage of T_4 points: (1,2) is covered by L1' (1,2) is on y=x+1? (1,2) yes. (2,2) is on L2'? (2,2) is on y=-2x+6? -4+6=2, yes. (3,2) is on L3'? (3,2) is on y=-1/2*3+3.5 = -1.5+3.5=2, yes. (1,3) is on L3'? (1,3) gives -0.5+3.5=3, yes. (2,3) is on L1'? (2,3) gives 2+1=3, yes. (1,4) is on L2'? (1,4) gives -2+6=4, yes. So all six points are covered!  Great.  So the construction for k=3 is:

Take the n-3 horizontal lines y = 1,2,вЂ¦,n-3 (nonвЂ‘sunny).  Then take the three sunny lines:

\[
\begin{aligned}
\ell_1 &: y = x+1,\\
\ell_2 &: y = -2x + (n+3),\\
\ell_3 &: y = -\frac12 x + \frac{n+3}{2}.
\end{aligned}
\]

But need to check that for n=3, n-3 = 0, so there are no horizontals; the three sunny lines become:

в„“1: y = x+1
в„“2: y = -2x + 6
в„“3: y = -1/2 x + 3

These cover all points of S_3.  So indeed, k=3 is achievable for all nв‰Ґ3.

Thus we have constructions for k=0,1,3.

---

### 2.  Proving that k cannot be 2

Assume that there exists a set of n distinct lines covering S_n with exactly 2 sunny lines.  We will derive a contradiction.

Let the sunny lines be L and M.  The other n-2 lines are nonвЂ‘sunny, so each is horizontal, vertical, or has slope -1.

Consider the points of S_n that are not covered by any nonвЂ‘sunny line; they must be covered by L and M.  The complement of the union of the nonвЂ‘sunny lines is some set T вЉ† S_n.  The number of points in T is at least (n-2)(n-1)/2?  Hmm, we need a lower bound on |T|.  Since there are only n-2 nonвЂ‘sunny lines, the maximum number of points they can cover is limited.  The best coverage is achieved by taking all n-2 lines to be horizontal lines with the largest possible sizes.  The horizontal lines are of the form y = b.  The size of horizontal line y = b is n+1-b.  So if we take the first t = n-2 horizontals (y = 1,вЂ¦,n-2), they cover:

\[
\sum_{b=1}^{n-2} (n+1-b) = (n-2)(n+1) - \frac{(n-2)(n-1)}{2}.
\]

The complement (points not covered by these horizontals) is the triangle of size n - (n-2) = 2, i.e., the points with b в‰Ґ n-1.  That triangle has size:

\[
\frac{2\cdot3}{2}=3.
\]

But is this the minimal possible complement?  We need a lower bound on |T|.  Since the nonвЂ‘sunny lines can be any combination, the complement size is minimized when the nonвЂ‘sunny lines cover as many points as possible.  The maximum coverage by t lines is achieved by taking the t lines of the type that cover the most points, i.e., horizontals (or verticals, or diagonals).  But we must also consider that the nonвЂ‘sunny lines are not required to be all of the same type; they could be a mix, but mixing may reduce coverage.  So the maximum coverage is attained by taking the t lines that are all horizontals with the largest sizes.  Therefore, for any set of t nonвЂ‘sunny lines, the number of points they cover is в‰¤ the sum of sizes of the t largest horizontals.  Consequently, the number of points **not** covered by them is в‰Ґ N - (that sum).  That's the size of the triangle of side (n - t).  Here t = n-2, so n - t = 2, giving a triangle of size 2, which has 3 points.  So we have:

\[
|T| \ge \frac{2\cdot3}{2}=3.
\]

Thus the complement T has at least 3 points.

Now, we need to cover these at least 3 points by the two sunny lines L and M.  However, a sunny line can intersect any given line of the form x = constant or y = constant or x+y = constant in at most 1 point.  But more importantly, consider the set T.  Since T is a subset of S_n, it is contained in the triangle.  The maximum number of points from T that can lie on a single sunny line is limited by the geometry of S_n.  But we need a stronger argument: we can show that any sunny line can contain at most 1 point from T, because T is a triangle of size at least 2 (if it's exactly size 2, that's 3 points).  But is it possible that a sunny line contains 2 points from T
